Here are a few ways GPT-3 can go wrong | TechCrunch
2019
https://techcrunch.com/2020/08/07/here-are-a-few-ways-gpt-3-can-go-wrong

Here are a few ways GPT-3 can go wrong 3 years Liz O'Sullivan Contributor More posts by this contributor Here are a few ways GPT-3 can go wrong John P. Dickerson Contributor OpenAI’s latest language generation model, GPT-3, has made quite the splash within AI circles, astounding reporters to the point where even Sam Altman, OpenAI’s leader, mentioned on Twitter that it may be overhyped. Still, there is no doubt that GPT-3 is powerful. Those with early-stage access to OpenAI’s GPT-3 API have shown how to translate natural language into code for websites , solve complex medical question-and-answer problems, create basic tabular financial reports , and even write code to train machine learning models — all with just a few well-crafted examples as input (i.e., via “ few-shot learning ”).
Soon, anyone will be able to purchase GPT-3’s generative power to make use of the language model, opening doors to build tools that will quietly (but significantly) shape our world. Enterprises aiming to take advantage of GPT-3, and the increasingly powerful iterations that will surely follow, must take great care to ensure that they install extensive guardrails when using the model, because of the many ways that it can expose a company to legal and reputational risk. Before we discuss some examples of how the model can potentially do wrong in practice, let’s first look at how GPT-3 was made.
Machine learning models are only as good, or as bad, as the data fed into them during training. In the case of GPT-3, that data is massive.
GPT-3 was trained on the Common Crawl dataset , a broad scrape of the 60 million domains on the internet along with a large subset of the sites to which they link. This means that GPT-3 ingested many of the internet’s more reputable outlets — think the BBC or The New York Times — along with the less reputable ones — think Reddit. Yet, Common Crawl makes up just 60% of GPT-3’s training data; OpenAI researchers also fed in other curated sources such as Wikipedia and the full text of historically relevant books.
Language models learn which succeeding words, phrases and sentences are likely to come next for any given input word or phrase. By “reading” text during training that is largely written by us, language models such as GPT-3 also learn how to “write” like us, complete with all of humanity’s best and worst qualities. Tucked away in the GPT-3 paper’s supplemental material, the researchers give us some insight into a small fraction of the problematic bias that lurks within. Just as you’d expect from any model trained on a largely unfiltered snapshot of the internet, the findings can be fairly toxic.
Because there is so much content on the web sexualizing women, the researchers note that GPT-3 will be much more likely to place words like “naughty” or “sucked” near female pronouns, where male pronouns receive stereotypical adjectives like “lazy” or “jolly” at the worst. When it comes to religion, “Islam” is more commonly placed near words like “terrorism” while a prompt of the word “Atheism” will be more likely to produce text containing words like “cool” or “correct.” And, perhaps most dangerously, when exposed to a text seed that involves racial content involving Blackness, the output GPT-3 gives tends to be more negative than corresponding white- or Asian-sounding prompts.
Image Credits: Arthur (opens in a new window) How might this play out in a real-world use case of GPT-3? Let’s say you run a media company, processing huge amounts of data from sources all over the world. You might want to use a language model like GPT-3 to summarize this information , which many news organizations already do today. Some even go so far as to automate story creation, meaning that the outputs from GPT-3 could land directly on your homepage without any human oversight.
 If the model carries a negative sentiment skew against Blackness — as is the case with GPT-3 — the headlines on your site will also receive that negative slant. An AI-generated summary of a neutral news feed about Black Lives Matter would be very likely to take one side in the debate.
 It’s pretty likely to condemn the movement, given the negatively charged language that the model will associate with racial terms like “Black.” This, in turn, could alienate parts of your audience and deepen racial tensions around the country. At best, you’ll lose a lot of readers. At worst, the headline could spark more protest and police violence, furthering this cycle of national unrest.
OpenAI’s website also details an application in medicine, where issues of bias can be enough to prompt federal inquiries , even when the modelers’ intentions are good. Attempts to proactively detect mental illness or rare underlying conditions worthy of intervention are already at work in hospitals around the country. It’s easy to imagine a healthcare company using GPT-3 to power a chatbot — or even something as “simple” as a search engine — that takes in symptoms from patients and outputs a recommendation for care. Imagine, if you will, a female patient suffering from a gynecological issue. The model’s interpretation of your patient’s intent might be married to other, less medical associations, prompting the AI to make offensive or dismissive comments , while putting her health at risk. The paper makes no mention of how the model treats at-risk minorities such as those who identify as transgender or nonbinary, but if the Reddit comments section is any indication of the responses we will soon see, the cause for worry is real.
But because algorithmic bias is rarely straightforward, many GPT-3 applications will act as canaries in the growing coal mine that is AI-driven applications. As COVID-19 ravages our nation, schools are searching for new ways to manage remote grading requirements, and the private sector has supplied solutions to take in schoolwork and output teaching suggestions. An algorithm tasked with grading essays or student reports is very likely to treat language from various cultures differently. Writing styles and word choice can vary significantly between cultures and genders. A GPT-3-powered paper-grader without guardrails might think that white-written reports are more worthy of praise, or it may penalize students based on subtle cues that indicate English as a second language, which are in turn, largely correlated to race. As a result, children of immigrants and from racial minorities will be less likely to graduate from high school, through no fault of their own.
The creators of GPT-3 plan to continue their research into the model’s biases, but for now, they simply surface these concerns, passing along the risk to any company or individual who’s willing to take the chance. All models are biased, as we know, and this should not be a reason to outlaw all AI, because its benefits can surely outweigh the risks in the long term. But in order to enjoy these benefits, we must ensure that as we rush to deploy powerful AI like GPT-3 to the enterprise, that we take sufficient precautions to understand, monitor for and act quickly to mitigate its points of failure. It’s only through a responsible combination of human and automated oversight that AI applications can be trusted to deliver societal value while protecting the common good.
This article was written by humans.

Those troubles compounded during the COVID pandemic when\u00a0<\/span> "font-weight: 400;\">companies abandoned office space and employees began working remotely. Even as some companies have returned to the office, the appetite for WeWork space didn’t rebound to those pre-pandemic days.<\/span><\/p>\n "font-weight: 400;\">Earlier this month, WeWork missed interest payments to its bondholders, and was granted 30 days to come up with those payments, according to <\/span> "https:\/\/d18rn0p25nwr6d.cloudfront.net\/CIK-0001813756\/75fb45d7-d40d-4f34-95e0-51188ec72bb8.pdf\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"> "font-weight: 400;\">a securities filing<\/span><\/a> "font-weight: 400;\">. On October 30, WeWork said it had begun discussions with \u201ccertain stakeholders in its capital structure\u201d such as SoftBank and Goldman Sachs about improving its balance sheet as it took steps \u201cto rationalize its real estate footprint.\u201d<\/span><\/p>\n "font-weight: 400;\">In August, the 13-year-old company announced a net loss of $397 million for the second quarter on revenue of $877 million. While revenue was up 4% year-over-year, WeWork interim CEO David Tolley noted in a statement at the time: \u201cExcess supply in commercial real estate, increasing competition in flexible space and macroeconomic volatility drove higher member churn and softer demand than we anticipated, resulting in a slight decline in memberships.\u201d<\/span><\/p>\n "font-weight: 400;\">The company\u2019s stock was <\/span> "https:\/\/finance.yahoo.com\/quote\/WE\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"> "font-weight: 400;\">down over 47% after-hours today<\/span><\/a> "font-weight: 400;\">, trading at just $1.21 and hitting a new 52-week low. h.com\/2023\/10\/31\/google-and-match-group-reach-settlement-in-app-store-antitrust-case\/","title":{"rendered":"Google and Match Group reach settlement in app store antitrust case"},"content":{"rendered":" "speakable-summary\">Google and Match announced today they’ve reached a settlement in the "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2023\/01\/19\/epic-and-matchs-antitrust-case-against-google-heads-to-jury-trial-on-november-6\/\">antitrust battle Match waged against the tech giant,<\/a> even as the court case continues with Fortnite maker Epic Games. As part of the settlement agreement, Match Group — which owns dating apps Tinder, Hinge, OKCupid, Match.com and others — will be able to implement “ "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/09\/02\/google-will-allow-alternative-payment-systems-for-play-store-in-more-countries\/\">user choice billing<\/a>” by March 31, 2024. This feature allows users to pay with other systems besides Google’s own.<\/p>\n Spotify and Bumble were among the initial testers for the new system, "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/11\/10\/google-play-to-pilot-third-party-billing-in-new-markets-including-u-s-bumble-joins-spotify-as-early-tester\/\">first introduced in November 2022<\/a>. Since then, Spotify’s third-party billing option "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2023\/01\/31\/spotifys-third-party-billing-option-has-now-reached-over-140-global-markets\/\"> rolled out to worldwide markets<\/a> as Google expanded user choice billing to "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/11\/10\/google-play-to-pilot-third-party-billing-in-new-markets-including-u-s-bumble-joins-spotify-as-early-tester\/\">more countries outside the U.S.<\/a><\/p>\n The program, so far, has not allowed third-party app markers that charge users for purchases and subscriptions to do away entirely with the commission Google requires. Instead, it offers a small percentage off the standard 15% to 30% commission. Currently, that discount is 4%, "https:\/\/support.google.com\/googleplay\/android-developer\/answer\/12570971\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">per Google’s help documentation.<\/a> The program is also still a pilot open to all developers globally who are selling apps to users in the supported 35 markets where it’s now available. Google has not said when the program will become globally available.<\/p>\n As part of the agreement, Match Group will not be allowed to only<\/em> offer its own billing system — it will offer its system alongside the existing Google Play billing system. Users will be able to choose which way they want to pay.<\/p>\n In addition, the settlement agreement includes a provision that Google will continue to work with Match Group in other areas where the two companies partner, including in Google Cloud and in the use of Google’s AI technologies, a Google spokesperson said. This suggests that the rift between the two tech companies incurred by the lawsuit could have led to more complicated relationships across other areas of both Google and Match’s business.<\/p>\n Like Apple, Google has long stressed that its commission structure is not only based on its ability to process payments for its customers. Instead, it suggests that the fees help to support the entire Google Play and Android app ecosystem, including hosting, app discovery, developer tools and other resources necessary to create an app business.<\/p>\n But as larger companies like Match and Epic have argued, they don’t need the same resources as other developers. In fact, "https:\/\/www.fortnite.com\/mobile\/android\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">as Epic demonstrated<\/a>, they could self-distribute apps and manage their own payments. They believed they should then at least have the option to have a more direct relationship with their paying customers, and Google preventing this was an anti-competitive concern.<\/p>\n “We are pleased to reach a settlement agreement with Match Group,” a Google spokesperson said, in a prepared statement. “This ensures we can continue to provide our shared users the secure, seamless, and high-quality experience people expect from apps on Google Play while maintaining Google\u2019s ability to invest in the Android ecosystem and deliver value across an app\u2019s full lifecycle,” they added.<\/p>\n “Today, the Match plaintiffs and Google informed the court that they have reached a binding term sheet for a settlement of their respective claims against each other in the Match Group, LLC et al vs. Google LLC et al lawsuit. Under the terms, the $40 million placed in escrow will be returned to Match Group and no other amounts will be owed by the Match plaintiffs to Google relating to the claims in the lawsuit for the period ending December 31, 2023,” a Match Group statement added, shared alongside the release of its Q3 earnings results.<\/p>\n Recently, "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2023\/08\/11\/google-pushes-match-for-more-money-in-antitrust-battle-court-filing-states\/\"> Google had pushed Match Group for more money in escrow<\/a>, saying that the $40 million would not be enough to cover the missed payments. (Match had been allowed to escrow funds to cover commissions while the lawsuit was underway.)<\/p>\n Match noted that the parties agreed that by March 31, 2024, its apps will implement Google\u2019s User Choice Billing, reducing its commission payments from 15% and 30% to 11% and 26%, respectively.<\/p>\n “The parties will enter a new partnership agreement that will provide value exchange across their broad relationship, which we expect will essentially offset the additional costs that Match Group brands expect to incur over the three years starting in 2024 associated with the implementation and continued use of User Choice Billing in compliance with Google\u2019s payment policy during that period,” Match Group also said.<\/p>\n In addition to the settlement news, Match Group reported a 9% year-over-year increase in revenue in Q3 to $882 million, with Tinder’s portion up 11% to $509 million.<\/p>\n It’s unclear whether this settlement will prompt Epic Games to also reach an agreement with the tech giant. If it does not, it’s set to go to trial next week to present its arguments.<\/p>\n "embed breakout embed-oembed embed--techcrunch\">\n "wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"qGcXVnzmF8\"> "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2023\/01\/19\/epic-and-matchs-antitrust-case-against-google-heads-to-jury-trial-on-november-6\/\">Epic and Match’s antitrust case against Google heads to jury trial on November 6<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote>\n "wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" style=\"position: absolute; la-wins-fatal-autopilot-crash-jury-trial\/","title":{"rendered":"Tesla wins fatal Autopilot crash jury trial"},"content":{"rendered":" "speakable-summary\">A jury on Tuesday handed Tesla another win after siding with the automaker over allegations that its Autopilot advanced driver assistance system led to a death.<\/p>\n The case, which was being tried in the Superior Court of California, County of Riverside, was filed by two passengers who survived a 2019 crash and alleged that Tesla knew its product was defective. The two survivors sought $400 million in damages for the loss of the driver’s life, physical injury and mental anguish.<\/p>\n Tesla argued that the crash, which resulted in the death of the driver Micah Lee, was the result of human error \u2014 the same stance it’s taken in other Autopilot lawsuits.<\/p>\n While Tesla has won other lawsuits, including a California jury trial earlier this year that determined the automaker’s Autopilot system was not to blame for a 2019 crash. In that case, the "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2023\/04\/21\/tesla-wins-autopilot-crash-case-in-california\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">jury awarded no damages<\/a> to Los Angeles resident Justine Hsu, who sued Tesla in 2020 alleging negligence, fraud and breach of contract. The Molander v. Tesla case that wrapped up Tuesday was the first jury trial involving a fatality.<\/p>\n Tesla still faces a number of other lawsuits in California, including a wrongful death suit that was filed by the family of Walter Huang, an Apple engineer who died after his Tesla Model X with Autopilot engaged crashed into a highway median. The State of California Department of Transportation is also named in the lawsuit. That wrongful death lawsuit, filed in California Superior Court, County of Santa Clara, alleges that errors by Tesla\u2019s Autopilot driver assistance system caused the crash that killed Huang on March 23, 2018. Huang, who was 38, died when his 2017 Tesla Model X hit a highway barrier on Highway 101 in Mountain View, California. A jury trial is expected to begin next year in that case.<\/p>\n Tesla is also facing scrutiny from "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2023\/08\/29\/nhtsa-raises-more-concerns-about-teslas-autopilot-safety\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">federal and state regulators,<\/a> all of which are related to Autopilot and its upgraded version known as Full Self-Driving.<\/p>\n Tesla vehicles come standard with a driver-assistance system branded as Autopilot. For a $6,000 upgrade, owners can purchase Enhanced Autopilot, which includes several other features, including an active guidance system that navigates a car from a highway on-ramp to off-ramp, including interchanges and making lane changes.<\/p> "piano-inline-promo\"><\/div>\n For an\u00a0additional $12,000, owners can buy \u201cfull self-driving,\u201d or FSD \u2014 a feature that CEO\u00a0 "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/tag\/elon-musk\/\" data-mrf-link=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/tag\/elon-musk\/\">Elon Musk<\/a>\u00a0has promised for years will one day deliver full autonomous driving capabilities.<\/p>\n Tesla vehicles\u00a0 "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/05\/07\/tesla-refutes-elon-musks-timeline-on-full-self-driving\/\" data-mrf-link=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/05\/07\/tesla-refutes-elon-musks-timeline-on-full-self-driving\/\">are not self-driving<\/a>. Instead, FSD includes a number of automated driving features that still require the driver to be ready to take control at all times. It includes everything in Enhanced Autopilot and is supposed to handle steering on city streets and recognize and react to traffic lights and stop signs.<\/p>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":" A jury on Tuesday handed Tesla another win after siding with the automaker over allegations that its Autopilot advanced driver assistance system led to a death. The case, which was being tried in the Superior Court of California, County of Riverside, was filed by two passengers who survived a 2019 crash and alleged that Tesla ","title":{"rendered":"So long, Twitter Circles"},"content":{"rendered":" "speakable-summary\">If tic-tac-toe has taught us anything, it’s that Xs and circles cannot peacefully coexist.<\/p>\n X, the app formerly known as Twitter, has shut down the Circles feature, which allowed users to post to a small, exclusive audience. Like an Instagram Close Friends story, Circles — n\u00e9e Twitter Circles — offered a more intimate social media experience. In other words, you could overshare about your life to an audience of friends only, rather than the whole internet. And isn’t one of the greatest joys of the internet to emotionally shitpost without worrying about the consequences?<\/p>\n But, as the whims of the Circle reminded us, nothing we say on the internet is ever actually free from consequence. The feature worked almost all of the time. Almost. And anything less than 100% efficacy is not ideal for a feature that people used to share private thoughts with trusted friends.<\/p>\n As early as February, some users reported that their Circle tweets were "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2023\/02\/03\/twitter-circle-glitches-have-users-worried-about-privacy\/\">reaching a wider audience<\/a> than they were supposed to. These glitches showed up "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2023\/04\/10\/twitter-circle-bug-not-private\/\">again in April<\/a>, and X (then known as Twitter) "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2023\/05\/05\/twitter-confirms-circle-tweets-temporarily-were-not-private\/\">apologized<\/a> for the error in May, marking a rare moment of transparency for the company.<\/p>\n Even when Circle tweets were truly private, they often appeared not to be — the green banner denoting it as a Circle post only appeared some of the time (I had received some paranoid texts from friends who wondered if I knew I had posted about [redacted] on main, but nope, this was just the glitchy old bird app playing tricks on us).<\/p>\n "embed breakout embed-oembed embed--twitter\">\n "twitter-tweet\" data-width=\"550\" data-dnt=\"true\">\n "en\" dir=\"ltr\">I can't believe this website is going to take away my most treasured joke: my Twitter circle that only contains Tony Hawk.
"https:\/\/t.co\/P9pRF2HTIy\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">pic.twitter.com\/P9pRF2HTIy<\/a><\/p>\n — Giovanni Colantonio (@MarioPrime) "https:\/\/twitter.com\/MarioPrime\/status\/1719398305526861961?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">October 31, 2023<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote> "piano-inline-promo\"><\/div>\n "https:\/\/platform.twitter.com\/widgets.js\" charset=\"utf-8\"><\/script><\/div>\n But perhaps nothing is sadder than the loss of "https:\/\/twitter.com\/MarioPrime\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">one user<\/a>‘s “most treasured joke,” in which he posted to a Circle audience consisting only of Tony Hawk, because you could add people who didn’t follow you to your Circle. And they could not remove themselves.<\/p>\n Some users have expressed concern that their private musings would be made public, now that Circles are no longer. So far, this doesn’t seem to be the case, though you’re able to view your own Circle tweets on your profile. But on X, who knows when something will break, so if you have anything salacious up, it might be worth deleting.<\/p>\n It’s not clear why "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2023\/09\/21\/x-is-shutting-down-its-circle-feature-in-october\/\">X is sunsetting Circles<\/a> — perhaps it will come back as a paid benefit, like another beloved feature, "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2023\/08\/15\/x-formerly-twitter-makes-x-pro-formerly-tweetdeck-a-subscriber-only-product\/\">TweetDeck<\/a> (now called X Pro). But users will understandably be annoyed if they have to pay money to X to regain features they used to have for free.<\/p>\n "embed breakout embed-oembed embed--techcrunch\">\n "wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"i0QNe6bERD\"> "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2023\/04\/10\/twitter-circle-bug-not-private\/\">Twitter Circle tweets are not that private anymore<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote>\n ndered":"SBF\u2019s defense puts forth a 35-minute last-ditch effort to show his goodwill"},"content":{"rendered":" "speakable-summary\"> "featured__span-first-words\">As Sam Bankman-Fried<\/span> took the stand for the last time in his trial for fraud and money laundering, his main lawyer, Mark Cohen, returned to question him on a number of themes that prosecutors touched on in the past few days. But this time, he took a different approach, presenting the defendant as a person who was acting in good faith.<\/p>\n \u201cWe lived and died by having a better product than competitors,\u201d Bankman-Fried said of his "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/11\/11\/ftx-files-for-bankruptcy-ceo-sam-bankman-fried-steps-down\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">now-bankrupt crypto exchange<\/a>, FTX. Bankman-Fried owned majority stakes in both FTX and its sister trading firm, Alameda Research, but insisted he wanted the best for both.<\/p>\n Bankman-Fried has been "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2023\/10\/26\/sam-bankman-fried-takes-the-stand\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">testifying since Thursday afternoon<\/a>, and the prosecution has grilled him for the past few days as "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2023\/10\/30\/sam-bankman-fried-ftx-cross-examination\/\">part of their cross-examination<\/a>. On Tuesday, when the defense lawyers returned to reexamine him, he was swaying slightly on the stand, shoulders drawn down and forward. He spoke softly, but was more talkative than earlier in the day and on Monday when he was being questioned.<\/p>\n Contrary to the picture prosecutors tried to paint, Bankman-Fried insisted that he was not involved in the day-to-day trading operations of Alameda or its \u201ccore operations,\u201d but instead emphasized his concern for its leadership at the time, saying he wanted to be involved in many of its venture investments, decisions on hedging, and other areas.

O\u2019Sullivan is CEO of "http:\/\/www.getparity.ai\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Parity<\/a>, a platform that automates model risk and algorithmic governance for the enterprise. She also advises the Surveillance Technology Oversight Project and the Campaign to Stop Killer Robots on all things artificial intelligence.\t<\/div>\n\t\n\t\t "contributor-byline__more-articles\">\n\t\t "more-articles-title\">More posts by this contributor<\/span>\n\t\t "more-articles-list\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2020\/08\/07\/here-are-a-few-ways-gpt-3-can-go-wrong\/\">Here are a few ways GPT-3 can go wrong<\/a><\/li>\n\t\t\t\t\t<\/ul>\n\t<\/div>\n\t<\/div>\n\n "article__contributor-byline\">\n\t "contributor-byline__contributor\">\n\t\t "byline__author\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t "byline__author-name\">John P. Dickerson<\/span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t "byline__author-title\" style=\"display: block;\">Contributor<\/span>\n\t\t<\/div>\n\n\t\t\t\t "contributor__twitter\">\n\t\t\t "_blank\" href=\"https:\/\/twitter.com\/johnpdickerson\">\n\t\t\t\t\t "icon icon--x icon--white\" viewBox=\"0 0 20 20\" version=\"1.1\" aria-labelledby=\"title\">\n\t\t Share on X<\/title>\n\t\t "M 0.16 0.759 L 7.256 10.9 L 0.533 18.759 L 3.174 18.759 L 8.437 12.59 L 12.754 18.759 L 19.664 18.759 L 12.242 8.134 L 18.533 0.759 L 15.934 0.759 L 11.064 6.447 L 7.092 0.759 L 0.16 0.759 Z M 4 2.759 L 6.049 2.759 L 15.826 16.759 L 13.795 16.759 L 4 2.759 Z\" \/>\t<\/svg>\n\t\t\t\t<\/a>\n\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t<\/div>\n\n\t\t "contributor-byline__bio\">\n\t\tJohn P. Dickerson is chief scientist at "http:\/\/www.arthur.ai\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Arthur<\/a> as well as computer science faculty at the University of Maryland; he works broadly at the intersection of economics and computer science with a focus on using machine learning to balance fairness and efficiency in markets.\t<\/div>\n\t\n\t<\/div>\n<\/div> "speakable-summary\">OpenAI\u2019s latest language generation model, GPT-3, has made quite the splash within AI circles, astounding reporters to the point where even Sam Altman, OpenAI\u2019s leader, "https:\/\/twitter.com\/sama\/status\/1284922296348454913?lang=en\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">mentioned on Twitter<\/a> that it may be overhyped. Still, there is no doubt that GPT-3 is powerful. Those with early-stage access to OpenAI\u2019s GPT-3 API have shown how to translate natural language into "https:\/\/twitter.com\/jsngr\/status\/1287026808429383680\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">code for websites<\/a>, solve complex "https:\/\/twitter.com\/QasimMunye\/status\/1278750809094750211\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">medical question-and-answer<\/a> problems, create "https:\/\/twitter.com\/itsyashdani\/status\/1285695850300219392\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">basic tabular financial reports<\/a>, and even write "https:\/\/twitter.com\/mattshumer_\/status\/1287125015528341506\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">code to train machine learning models <\/a>\u2014 all with just a few well-crafted examples as input (i.e., via \u201c "https:\/\/medium.com\/quick-code\/understanding-few-shot-learning-in-machine-learning-bede251a0f67\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">few-shot learning<\/a>\u201d).<\/p>\n Soon, anyone will be able to purchase GPT-3\u2019s generative power to make use of the language model, opening doors to build tools that will quietly (but significantly) shape our world. Enterprises aiming to take advantage of GPT-3, and the increasingly powerful iterations that will surely follow, must take great care to ensure that they install extensive guardrails when using the model, because of the many ways that it can expose a company to "https:\/\/www.washingtonpost.com\/business\/2019\/11\/11\/apple-card-algorithm-sparks-gender-bias-allegations-against-goldman-sachs\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">legal<\/a> and "https:\/\/themarkup.org\/google-the-giant\/2020\/07\/23\/google-advertising-keywords-black-girls\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">reputational<\/a> risk. Before we discuss some examples of how the model can potentially do wrong in practice, let\u2019s first look at "https:\/\/arxiv.org\/pdf\/2005.14165.pdf\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">how GPT-3 was made<\/a>.<\/p>\n Machine learning models are only as good, or as bad, as the data fed into them during training. In the case of GPT-3, that data is massive.<\/em> GPT-3 was trained on the Common Crawl "https:\/\/commoncrawl.org\/the-data\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">dataset<\/a>, a broad scrape of the 60 million domains on the internet along with a large subset of the sites to which they link. This means that GPT-3 ingested many of the internet\u2019s more reputable outlets — think the BBC or The New York Times — along with the less reputable ones — think Reddit. Yet, Common Crawl makes up just 60% of GPT-3\u2019s training data; OpenAI researchers also fed in other curated sources such as Wikipedia and the full text of historically relevant books.<\/p>\n Language models learn which succeeding words, phrases and sentences are likely to come next for any given input word or phrase. By \u201creading\u201d text during training that is largely written by us, language models such as GPT-3 also learn how to \u201cwrite\u201d like us, complete with all of humanity\u2019s best and worst qualities. Tucked away in the GPT-3 paper\u2019s supplemental material, the researchers give us some insight into a small fraction of the problematic bias that lurks within. Just as you\u2019d expect from any model trained on a largely unfiltered snapshot of the internet, the findings can be fairly toxic.<\/p>\n Because there is so much content on the web sexualizing women, the researchers note that GPT-3 will be much more likely to place words like \u201cnaughty\u201d or \u201csucked\u201d near female pronouns, where male pronouns receive stereotypical adjectives like \u201clazy\u201d or \u201cjolly\u201d at the worst. When it comes to religion, \u201cIslam\u201d is more commonly placed near words like \u201cterrorism\u201d while a prompt of the word \u201cAtheism\u201d will be more likely to produce text containing words like \u201ccool\u201d or \u201ccorrect.\u201d And, perhaps most dangerously, when exposed to a text seed that involves racial content involving Blackness, the output GPT-3 gives tends to be more negative than corresponding white- or Asian-sounding prompts.<\/p>\n "attachment_2027929\" style=\"width: 1034px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"> "caption-attachment-2027929\" decoding=\"async\" loading=\"lazy\" class=\"size-full wp-image-2027929\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2020\/08\/biasedwords.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"458\" srcset=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2020\/08\/biasedwords.jpg 1119w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2020\/08\/biasedwords.jpg?resize=150,67 150w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2020\/08\/biasedwords.jpg?resize=300,134 300w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2020\/08\/biasedwords.jpg?resize=768,344 768w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2020\/08\/biasedwords.jpg?resize=680,304 680w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2020\/08\/biasedwords.jpg?resize=50,22 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" \/> "caption-attachment-2027929\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"> Image Credits:<\/strong> "https:\/\/www.arthur.ai\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Arthur "screen-reader-text\">(opens in a new window)<\/span><\/a><\/p><\/div><\/p>\n How might this play out in a real-world use case of GPT-3? Let\u2019s say you run a media company, processing huge amounts of data from sources all over the world. You might want to use a language model like GPT-3 to "https:\/\/venturebeat.com\/2018\/11\/06\/microsoft-researchers-develop-ai-system-that-can-generate-articles-summaries\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">summarize this information<\/a>, which many news organizations already do today. Some even go so far as to automate story creation, meaning that the outputs from GPT-3 could land directly on your homepage "https:\/\/www.theguardian.com\/technology\/2020\/jun\/09\/microsofts-robot-journalist-confused-by-mixed-race-little-mix-singers\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">without any human oversight<\/a>. If the model carries a negative sentiment skew against Blackness — as is the case with GPT-3 — the headlines on your site will also receive that negative slant. An AI-generated summary of a neutral news feed about Black Lives Matter would be very likely to "https:\/\/arstechnica.com\/information-technology\/2019\/02\/twenty-minutes-into-the-future-with-openais-deep-fake-text-ai\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">take one side in the debate<\/a>. It\u2019s pretty likely to condemn the movement, given the negatively charged language that the model will associate with racial terms like \u201cBlack.\u201d This, in turn, could alienate parts of your audience and deepen racial tensions around the country. At best, you\u2019ll lose a lot of readers. At worst, the headline could spark more protest and police violence, furthering this cycle of national unrest.<\/p> "piano-inline-promo\"><\/div>\n "https:\/\/beta.openai.com\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">OpenAI\u2019s website<\/a> also details an application in medicine, where issues of bias can be enough to prompt "https:\/\/www.wsj.com\/articles\/new-york-regulator-probes-unitedhealth-algorithm-for-racial-bias-11572087601\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">federal inquiries<\/a>, even when the modelers\u2019 intentions are good. Attempts to proactively detect "https:\/\/www.wsj.com\/articles\/pandemic-related-disruptions-hurt-reliability-of-some-ai-models-11591781402\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">mental illness<\/a> or rare underlying conditions worthy of intervention are already at work in hospitals around the country. It\u2019s easy to imagine a healthcare company using GPT-3 to power a chatbot — or even something as \u201csimple\u201d as a search engine — that takes in symptoms from patients and outputs a recommendation for care. Imagine, if you will, a female patient suffering from a gynecological issue. The model\u2019s interpretation of your patient\u2019s intent might be married to other, less medical associations, prompting the AI to make offensive or "https:\/\/qz.com\/1766418\/how-using-babylon-healths-ai-symptom-checker-could-go-wrong\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">dismissive comments<\/a>, while putting her health at risk. The paper makes no mention of how the model treats at-risk minorities such as those who identify as transgender or nonbinary, but if the Reddit "https:\/\/www.motherjones.com\/politics\/2019\/08\/reddit-hate-content-moderation\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">comments section<\/a> is any indication of the responses we will soon see, the cause for worry is real.<\/p>\n But because algorithmic bias is rarely straightforward, many GPT-3 applications will act as canaries in the growing coal mine that is AI-driven applications. As COVID-19 ravages our nation, schools are searching for new ways to manage remote grading requirements, and the private sector has supplied solutions to take in schoolwork and output teaching suggestions. An algorithm tasked with grading essays or student reports is very likely to treat language from various cultures differently. Writing styles and word choice can "https:\/\/www.vox.com\/recode\/2019\/8\/15\/20806384\/social-media-hate-speech-bias-black-african-american-facebook-twitter\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">vary significantly<\/a> between cultures and genders. A GPT-3-powered paper-grader without guardrails might think that white-written reports are more worthy of praise, or it may penalize students based on subtle cues that indicate English as a second language, which are in turn, largely correlated to race. As a result, children of immigrants and from racial minorities will be less likely to graduate from high school, through "https:\/\/www.wired.com\/story\/algorithm-set-students-grades-altered-futures\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">no fault of their own<\/a>.<\/p>\n The creators of GPT-3 plan to continue their research into the model\u2019s biases, but for now, they simply surface these concerns, passing along the risk to any company or individual who\u2019s willing to take the chance. All models are biased, as we know, and this should not be a reason to outlaw all AI, because its benefits can surely outweigh the risks in the long term. But in order to enjoy these benefits, we must ensure that as we rush to deploy powerful AI like GPT-3 to the enterprise, that we take sufficient precautions to understand, monitor for and act quickly to mitigate its points of failure. It\u2019s only through a responsible combination of human and "https:\/\/www.arthur.ai\/blog\/2019\/7\/19\/never-deploy-ai-without-doing-these-3-things\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">automated oversight<\/a> that AI applications can be trusted to deliver societal value while protecting the common good.<\/p>\n This article was written by humans<\/em>.<\/p>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":" Because algorithmic bias is rarely straightforward, many GPT-3 applications will act as canaries in the growing coal mine that is
